              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30817744-sandy-the-world-could-end-while-i-was-reading-and-i-would-never-notice?shelf=contemporary-fiction">contemporary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30817744-sandy-the-world-could-end-while-i-was-reading-and-i-would-never-notice?shelf=family-drama">family-drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30817744-sandy-the-world-could-end-while-i-was-reading-and-i-would-never-notice?shelf=favourite-author">favourite-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30817744-sandy-the-world-could-end-while-i-was-reading-and-i-would-never-notice?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30817744-sandy-the-world-could-end-while-i-was-reading-and-i-would-never-notice?shelf=purely-for-pleasure">purely-for-pleasure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30817744-sandy-the-world-could-end-while-i-was-reading-and-i-would-never-notice?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2133985806" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2777511445270994602">EXCERPT: What <i>had </i> gone into the making of Teddy? Not slugs and snails, it was true, but generation upon generation of Beresfords and Todds, all coming to one singular point in a cold bed in the chill of an autumn night when his father had caught hold of the golden rope of his mother's hair and hadn't let go until he had hauled them both to the far shore (they had many euphemisms for the act). As they lay amongst the shipwreck of the marital bed they each felt slightly befuddled by the unexpecte</span>
  <span id="freeText2777511445270994602" style="display:none">EXCERPT: What <i>had </i> gone into the making of Teddy? Not slugs and snails, it was true, but generation upon generation of Beresfords and Todds, all coming to one singular point in a cold bed in the chill of an autumn night when his father had caught hold of the golden rope of his mother's hair and hadn't let go until he had hauled them both to the far shore (they had many euphemisms for the act). As they lay amongst the shipwreck of the marital bed they each felt slightly befuddled by the unexpected ardor of the other. Hugh cleared his throat and murmured, 'A voyage into the deep, eh?' Sylvie said nothing as she felt the seafaring metaphor had been stretched far enough. <br /><br />But the grain had entered the shell (Sylvie's own metaphoric stance) and the pearl that would be Edward Beresford Todd began to grow until he was revealed into the sunshine that came before the Great War and lay happily for hours on end in his pram with nothing but a silver hare dangling from the pram hood for company. <br /><br />THE BLURB: In Life After Life Ursula Todd lived through the turbulent events of the last century again and again. In A God in Ruins, Atkinson turns her focus on Ursula’s beloved younger brother Teddy – would-be poet, RAF bomber pilot, husband and father – as he navigates the perils and progress of the 20th century. For all Teddy endures in battle, his greatest challenge will be to face living in a future he never expected to have.<br /><br />MY THOUGHTS: This is a complex book. There seems to be no order to it. It randomly jumps from Teddy's childhood, to his old age, to his war years and back, interspersed with the lives of his one daughter, Viola, and her two children, Sunny and Bertie, and back again. <br /><br />And yet, with her own inimitable style, Kate Atkinson pulls it off and rather splendidly at that. It is like sitting with a loved elderly relative, listening to them reminisce, where one memory leads to another, the tenuous thread that connects them known only to the narrator. And yet Atkinson draws you into this family. I laughed, I cried. I seethed at Viola's indifference to her children, her father. I flew with Teddy on his sorties over Germany, crossing my fingers to keep him safe. I applauded his rescue of Sunny (aka Philip Villiers) from the Villiers enclave, and his careful nurturing of Bertie. And I wept at his gentle decline in residential care. <br /><br />This is both a heart-wrenching and heartwarming read. <br /><br />All opinions expressed in this review are entirely my own. Please refer to my Goodreads.com profile page or the 'about' page on sandysbookaday.wordpress.com for an explanation of my rating system. This review and others are also published on my blog sandysbookaday.wordpress.com<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://wordpress.com/post/sandysbookaday.wordpress.com/505">https://wordpress.com/post/sandysbook...</a></span>

<div id="review_1445699834" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1445699834" />
    <a title="BrokenTune" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune"><img alt="BrokenTune" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391017756p2/10592271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1445699834?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BrokenTune" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BrokenTune" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune">BrokenTune</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10592271-brokentune?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1445699834" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18292780748081155244">I DNF'd this book last night.<br/><br/>I was so bored with A God in Ruins that I skipped the second half of the book and advanced to the end of the book.<br/><br/>There is a twist at the end of the story, but it was too little and too late, and I had kind of predicted it anyway.<br/><br/>As with Life After Life, it was not the writing that put me off. Kate Atkinson clearly has a talent for writing beautiful prose. My problem was again with the plot.<br/><br/>I found it very hard to get invested in the story of Teddy, the brother of Ur</span>
  <span id="freeText18292780748081155244" style="display:none">I DNF'd this book last night.<br /><br />I was so bored with A God in Ruins that I skipped the second half of the book and advanced to the end of the book.<br /><br />There is a twist at the end of the story, but it was too little and too late, and I had kind of predicted it anyway.<br /><br />As with Life After Life, it was not the writing that put me off. Kate Atkinson clearly has a talent for writing beautiful prose. My problem was again with the plot.<br /><br />I found it very hard to get invested in the story of Teddy, the brother of Ursula from Life After Life.<br /><br />A God in Ruins is not one of those books that is driven by plot, and so it relies on its characters being interesting enough to keep reading. While Teddy's daughter, Viola, could have provided this interest, Teddy didn't. I get that Teddy was a representation of the many WW2 pilots who returned from the war to lead very ordinary lives without fuss and much excitement, and I appreciate that Teddy is a tribute to those men. However, I would rather read about these men from non-fiction sources than in a fictional environment.<br /><br />As for the point of the book that events and lives are interlinked and a change in one results in a ripple effect that will change others, I believe this came across quite well in Life After Life and did not need the additional elaboration of Teddy's story in A God in Ruins.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18292780748081155244" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1187130025" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1187130025" />
    <a title="Julie Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine"><img alt="Julie Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445900882p2/1213607.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1187130025?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Christine" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine">Julie Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=contemporary-fiction">contemporary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=read-2015">read-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=british-isles-theme-setting">british-isles-theme-setting</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=war-conflict">war-conflict</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1187130025" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16772599804282167312">“The whole edifice of civilization turned out to be constructed from an unstable mix of quicksand and imagination.” <br/>― Kate Atkinson, A God in Ruins<br/><br/>"It's still the same old story<br/>A fight for love and glory<br/>A case of do or die<br/>The world will always welcome lovers<br/>As time goes by"<br/>-Frank Sinatra, As Time Goes By<br/><br/>In the 2013 déjà-vu epic <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/527900001' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Life After Life</i>
</a> Kate Atkinson played out <i>"what if?"</i> in a loop-de-loop plot that tossed narrative structure to the wind. It is a brilliant, confounding, playful and po</span>
  <span id="freeText16772599804282167312" style="display:none">“The whole edifice of civilization turned out to be constructed from an unstable mix of quicksand and imagination.” <br />― Kate Atkinson, A God in Ruins<br /><br />"It's still the same old story<br />A fight for love and glory<br />A case of do or die<br />The world will always welcome lovers<br />As time goes by"<br />-Frank Sinatra, As Time Goes By<br /><br />In the 2013 déjà-vu epic <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/527900001" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Life After Life</i>
</a> Kate Atkinson played out <i>"what if?"</i> in a loop-de-loop plot that tossed narrative structure to the wind. It is a brilliant, confounding, playful and poignant novel. <i>A God In Ruins</i> returns us to Fox Corner and the Todd family of <i>Life After Life</i>, but aware that clever conceit is best eaten while warm, Atkinson returns to a more conventional structure for this "companion" novel. Sort of. <br /><br /><i>A God In Ruins</i> plucks Teddy Todd from Ursula Todd's war and follows his life, from RAF bomber during WWII to his final days in a nursing home in the 21st century. The trajectory is not chronological—the narrative leaps back and forth between the skies over Hamburg to Teddy's prosaic, duty-bound post-war life, foreshadowing and reflecting back on itself—but unlike <i>Life After Life</i>, Teddy lives but one life (I feel it necessary to say that although <i>A God In Ruins</i> is a standalone novel, you'd be doing yourself a terrible disservice by not reading <i>Life After Life</i> first. Not only is it a phenomenal read, but you will enter <i>A God In Ruins</i> already knowing and loving these characters and appreciating the winks and nods blithely scattered in the text).<br /><br />While still presenting the artifice of fiction—in the Author's Note, Atkinson states that fiction is essentially "how we must imagine what we cannot know"—<i>A God In Ruins</i> is about young men and war. It is an homage to the many who do not survive and the story of how it leaves in ruins those young gods who do. The novel is built on Teddy's flashes of memory and in this way, it carries forward the sense of déjà-vu that made <i>Life After Life</i> such a tour de force. But <i>Ruins</i> is quieter, except for the breathless scenes of bomber raids that Teddy leads. The reader never knows who will return alive (90 percent of the young men who joined the WWII Bomber Command did not) and the drama is rendered in extraordinary, fever-pitch detail. <br /><br />Contrasting Teddy's Greatest Generation heroics is the ridiculousness of his daughter, Viola, who Atkinson writes in caricature, almost unfairly at times. It's impossible not to laugh or smirk at Viola's irrelevant, hapless life, except when you are bemoaning her tragic neglect of her children, son Sunny and daughter Bertie (and Atkinson indulges in some terrific metafiction moments, offered like sticky Turkish Delight—Viola shuffles from indulgent hippy to pampered bestselling writer, her chipped shoulder leading the way). In a quieter display of domestic heroics, Teddy steps in as dutiful, if not overtly tender, grandfather. There is a thick scar of tension running between Viola and her father, slowly revealed as the courtship and marriage of Teddy and Viola's mother, Nancy, unfolds.<br /><br />I still come cross readers who have never heard of Atkinson (whaaaaa???) or dismiss her because of the supposed-genre Jackson Brodie series. I can press her books into the hand of the innocently ignorant; the willfully ignorant don't merit the breath. Kate Atkinson is one of the finest, most versatile, humane and intelligent storytellers of our generation. Dismiss her at your own peril and loss.<br /><br />If <i>Life After Life</i> is about beginning and beginning again, <i>A God In Ruins</i> is about our inevitable end. And speaking of endings, well, no, I won't. I can't. Just don't mind me for sinking to the floor, clutching at the pieces of my broken heart. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16772599804282167312" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2073096514" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2073096514" />
    <a title="Heidi The Reader" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7986781-heidi-the-reader"><img alt="Heidi The Reader" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593309467p2/7986781.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2073096514?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heidi The Reader" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heidi The Reader" href="/user/show/7986781-heidi-the-reader">Heidi The Reader</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7986781-heidi-the-reader?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7986781-heidi-the-reader?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2073096514" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14740451466657099141">Even though Kate Atkinson took readers back into the beautiful world that she created for the Todd family, this story wasn't nearly as enjoyable as <i>Life After Life.</i><br/><br/>This time, the story focused on Teddy. It is told through the mixed up timeline that I've come to expect from Atkinson. We get to see Teddy's relationships, family and inner thoughts.<br/><br/>It didn't have the magic of Ursula's story, in my opinion. In <i>Life After Life</i>, I was enthralled. For the majority of <i>A God in Ruins</i>, I was not.<br/><br/>I was surp</span>
  <span id="freeText14740451466657099141" style="display:none">Even though Kate Atkinson took readers back into the beautiful world that she created for the Todd family, this story wasn't nearly as enjoyable as <i>Life After Life.</i><br /><br />This time, the story focused on Teddy. It is told through the mixed up timeline that I've come to expect from Atkinson. We get to see Teddy's relationships, family and inner thoughts.<br /><br />It didn't have the magic of Ursula's story, in my opinion. In <i>Life After Life</i>, I was enthralled. For the majority of <i>A God in Ruins</i>, I was not.<br /><br />I was surprised that I liked very few of the characters. Viola, in particular, was awful. I realize that that is partially the point, but still- it's hard to appreciate the story when you don't like most of the major characters.<br /><br />The writing was still lovely, but I didn't connect to this book the way that I did with the other one. I'm rather disappointed actually.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14740451466657099141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10662626698000722803" style="display:none"><br />"A man is a God in ruins. When men are innocent, life shall be longer, and shall pass into the immortal,as gently as we awake from dreams" - Ralph Waldo Emerson<br /><br />In this companion piece to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life" title="Life After Life by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Life After Life</a> Atkinson writes about Teddy Todd: beloved younger brother of Ursula, would-be poet, husband, father, grandfather, World War II bomber pilot. In it, she breathes life into themes that are both extraordinary and mundane: the shortness and fragility of life, the certainty of death, the fall from grace that is war, the redemptive power of love, the artifice and reality of fiction. All are dealt in Atkinson's intelligent and quirky style.<br /><br />I fell in love with Teddy and I wept for all young people whose lives are ruined by war and who miss out on what otherwise could have been. Atkinson made me laugh and she made me cry. She made me think and she left me profoundly shaken. How could I ask for anything more from a novel?<br /><br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15674018833188557052">I regret so much that I never got back to write a review close to when I finished reading this book. It's unfair to my reading experience, the book and the author. But I didn't want to leave this spot empty forever so I will add a bit. For those who have read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life' title='Life After Life by Kate Atkinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Life After Life</a>, I definitely recommend this. For those who haven't, it likely will stand alone but will lose some of it's meaning because of the inter-related stories. <br/><br/>I am going to include what I wrote in a late status update when I finis</span>
  <span id="freeText15674018833188557052" style="display:none">I regret so much that I never got back to write a review close to when I finished reading this book. It's unfair to my reading experience, the book and the author. But I didn't want to leave this spot empty forever so I will add a bit. For those who have read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life" title="Life After Life by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Life After Life</a>, I definitely recommend this. For those who haven't, it likely will stand alone but will lose some of it's meaning because of the inter-related stories. <br /><br />I am going to include what I wrote in a late status update when I finished the book: What a ride Atkinson takes us on. One of my early text notes spoke to Teddy reliving his life figuratively while Ursula relived it in reality. Now I must amend this a bit. I see Ursula's life as integration and Teddy's as disintegration: so many forces at play on him. I won't go into that further but I will say this was a very satisfying novel, if not quite as spectacular as Life After Life.<br /><br />Atkinson is one of my favorite authors. She takes risks with her characters and her readers. I will read her next book and have some of her backlog to catch up with (thankfully).<br /><br />I rate this 4.5*, just slightly behind Life After Life.</span>

<div id="review_1297264536" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1297264536" />
    <a title="Gary  the Bookworm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7036337-gary-the-bookworm"><img alt="Gary  the Bookworm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491094498p2/7036337.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1297264536?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary  the Bookworm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary  the Bookworm" href="/user/show/7036337-gary-the-bookworm">Gary  the Bookworm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1297264536&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1297264536" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4520142665318529328">Teddy Todd is a character from Kate Atkinson's earlier novel <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life' title='Life After Life by Kate Atkinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Life After Life</a>. He is the beloved baby brother, and favorite son at Fox Corner, the Todd family home in the London suburbs. He grows up to be a bomber pilot in World War II and is either killed on a risky mission toward the end of the war or goes on to live a long life as a husband, father and grandfather. Atkinson considers both scenarios, but this novel focuses on the latter. She calls this a companion piece to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life' title='Life After Life by Kate Atkinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Life After Life</a>, whic</span>
  <span id="freeText4520142665318529328" style="display:none">Teddy Todd is a character from Kate Atkinson's earlier novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life" title="Life After Life by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Life After Life</a>. He is the beloved baby brother, and favorite son at Fox Corner, the Todd family home in the London suburbs. He grows up to be a bomber pilot in World War II and is either killed on a risky mission toward the end of the war or goes on to live a long life as a husband, father and grandfather. Atkinson considers both scenarios, but this novel focuses on the latter. She calls this a companion piece to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life" title="Life After Life by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Life After Life</a>, which is about Ursula, Teddy's older sister, and her multiple lives. I fell in love with all the Todds, so I approached this with high expectations. <br /><br />Whereas, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life" title="Life After Life by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Life After Life</a> is about life's possibilities, this is fundamentally about making the best of the vicissitudes of the ordinary. Teddy "had been reconciled to death during the war and then suddenly the war was over and there was a next day and a next day and a next day. Part of him never adjusted to having a future." If he was powerless to shape his future, he was determined to live his life with integrity and "he resolved that he would try always to be kind. It was the best he could do. It was all that he could do. And it might be love, after all." <br /><br />The title comes from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12080.Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson" rel="nofollow noopener">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a>: “A man is a god in ruins. When men are innocent, life shall be longer, and shall pass into the immortal, as gently as we awake from dreams.” Flight is a recurring theme. When Teddy and Ursula attend a performance of Beethoven's Ninth in war-torn London, "Teddy resolved to simply feel the music and stopped searching for words to describe it, and by the time the fourth movement came around and Roy Henderson, the baritone, began to sing ( O Freude! ), the hairs on the back of his neck were standing up. In her seat beside him, Ursula was almost quivering with the power of emotion, like a coiled spring, a bird ready to rise from the ground at any moment. Towards the end of the final movement, when the magnificence of the Choral becomes almost unbearable, Teddy had the odd sensation that he might actually have to hold on to his sister to prevent her rising into the air and taking flight." <br /><br />Passages like this suggest to me that Atkinson has been touched by the divine. If she never wrote another word, this novel should assure her place in the pantheon of World Literature. <br /><br /><br /> <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.npr.org/2015/06/08/411696701/bombs-blast-and-time-marches-on-in-a-god-in-ruins">http://www.npr.org/2015/06/08/4116967...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4520142665318529328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1140072358" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1140072358" />
    <a title="Joanne Harris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34428697-joanne-harris"><img alt="Joanne Harris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442439458p2/34428697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1140072358?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joanne Harris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joanne Harris" href="/user/show/34428697-joanne-harris">Joanne Harris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385618700" href="/book/show/23346581-a-god-in-ruins">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1140072358" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13830296975735888526">	<br/>I received an ARC of this from the publisher. The cover of the book reads: What if the new Kate Atkinson were even better than the last? Well, A God in Ruins is in some ways similar to Life After Life. It’s a companion piece, built along a similarly complex timeline and focusing on the life of Ursula Todd’s younger brother Teddy; his part in the War; his marriage; his reluctant navigation of the perilous, shifting waters of the twentieth century. Like all Kate Atkinson’s work, it is a masterly </span>
  <span id="freeText13830296975735888526" style="display:none">	<br />I received an ARC of this from the publisher. The cover of the book reads: What if the new Kate Atkinson were even better than the last? Well, A God in Ruins is in some ways similar to Life After Life. It’s a companion piece, built along a similarly complex timeline and focusing on the life of Ursula Todd’s younger brother Teddy; his part in the War; his marriage; his reluctant navigation of the perilous, shifting waters of the twentieth century. Like all Kate Atkinson’s work, it is a masterly piece of writing; the prose is clear and elegant; sometimes wryly humorous; often touching and with a wistful awareness of passing time that underpins the narrative with a constant sense of melancholy. <br />	There is, however, an odd disparity between the portrayal of the characters – those of the older generation are lovingly and richly drawn, while the younger ones (and most of the minor characters) are often prone to caricature and stereotype. We have characters coded according to accent, region, hair colour, social class and the kind of newspaper they read; and Viola, Teddy’s daughter, embodies every hippie cliché there is (Vegetarianism, life in a commune, homespun clothing, lentils, a couple of children named Sun and Moon). There’s also a tendency to characterize through little asides, helpfully delivered by other members of the cast, as if anticipating questions from an eventual readers’ group. Thus, at one point, Teddy asks himself if his assessment of his daughter’s personality counts as cod-psychology (it does); later, he muses, rather self-consciously, that Viola’s difficult character may have been shaped by her mother’s early death (it has). It is a kind of ironic wink to the reader – but that irony is also the means of keeping us at a distance; of reminding us that we are simply reading fragments of narrative on a page. I love Kate Atkinson’s writing. It is beautiful and perfectly-pitched - but there is an emotional detachment to her narrative that I have always found a little frustrating. To me, it often seems aloof, teasing the emotions, but deliberately withdrawing the hand just before it reaches the heart. Brilliant but (this may be my fault) ultimately, leaving me cold.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13830296975735888526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1304913177" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1304913177" />
    <a title="Helle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6909470-helle"><img alt="Helle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430669590p2/6909470.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1304913177?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Helle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Helle" href="/user/show/6909470-helle">Helle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385618700" href="/book/show/23346581-a-god-in-ruins">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=english">english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1304913177" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15076731263582756085">This novel is why I read.<br/><br/>I first met Kate Atkinson in <i>Life after Life</i>, which, while not quite being a five-star read for me, made me want to explore her work further. <i>A God in Ruins</i>, a companion to <i>Life after Life</i>, revisits the same characters and adds layers of story and understanding to the lives of the Todd family. (You do not have to read <i>Life after Life</i> first; they are not chronologically sequenced). <br/><br/>The heart of this novel is Teddy, an RAF bomber pilot in World War II, the sister of Ursula</span>
  <span id="freeText15076731263582756085" style="display:none">This novel is why I read.<br /><br />I first met Kate Atkinson in <i>Life after Life</i>, which, while not quite being a five-star read for me, made me want to explore her work further. <i>A God in Ruins</i>, a companion to <i>Life after Life</i>, revisits the same characters and adds layers of story and understanding to the lives of the Todd family. (You do not have to read <i>Life after Life</i> first; they are not chronologically sequenced). <br /><br />The heart of this novel is Teddy, an RAF bomber pilot in World War II, the sister of Ursula in <i>Life after Life</i>, the father of Viola and grandfather of Sonny and Bertie/Roberta. They are our central players in this novel which weaves back and forth in time, letting a scene in the future unfold here, going back into the past and filling in a bit of backstory there – like a giant patchwork gradually taking shape. <br /><br />The novel is above all rich, and there is so much to love in it. Here is my incomplete list (which includes both ‘items’ from the story and the author’s tools):<br /><br />Teddy’s love for Sonny<br /><br />The dogs<br /><br />The depiction of Sonny’s childhood (raw, ruthless but oh, so honest)<br /><br />Teddy’s interlude with Julia and her family’s art (and the loss of a Rembrandt)<br /><br />The relationship between Teddy and his flight crew<br /><br />Viola’s regrets, ultimately<br /><br />Atkinson’s sardonic comments sprinkled throughout a narrative that is fundamentally warm<br /><br />Her knowing insights into family dynamics<br /><br />Her allusions and direct references to numerous other works of literature, incl. <i>Bleak House, <br />Anna Karenina, Little Women, Anne of Green Gables</i>, Hans Christian Andersen’s <i>The Wild <br />Swans, A Room with a View, Barchester Towers, The Pilgrim’s Progress</i><br /><br />But most of all the novel’s holy trinity of humour, heart and intelligence; or plot, prose and <br />characters – plausible, flawed and multifaceted characters – all serving the higher purpose of a <br />narrative whose themes and images are set in motion by the same characters. Like Teddy’s bomber <br />itself, character and theme are sent spinning into the air and only gradually (in the beginning, the <br />lack of chronology was confusing) form a pattern, the jigsaw finally forming what was – <br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[or could have been <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> - a life. <br /><br />What I didn’t like: <br /><br />The cover. It doesn’t half measure up to the beautiful cover of my copy of <i>Life after Life</i> <br />and, more importantly, belies the richness of the book itself. But I suppose that is a detail. And it was the only thing I didn't like.<br /><br />There were times when the way Atkinson uses language reminded me of Julian Barnes’s writing, <br />for instance in her repetition of images and thematic phrases like <i>The dead were legion. (The <br />gods had their own secret agenda) + (…) and so they continued throwing the birds against the wall, <br />and still the wall stood</i>, the latter a recantation of the act of killing during the war – and its <br />hopeless contribution to a solution. (Another repeated, concrete symbol was a small, silver hare <br />with an ivory teething ring attached to it, which touches several of the Todd’s lives and which <br />Teddy carries with him as a talisman when he is on his ‘tours’ (bombing raids). This touched me <br />because, incredibly, I had one such silver hare with an ivory teething ring when I was a baby). <br /><br />The novel is about the devastations of wars, of World War II on its participants; of growing up and <br />growing old, of joys and disappointments, but ultimately it is about fiction, the fiction of stories, the fiction of our lives and what we make of them in the face of atrocities – war, illness, family <br />disillusionment. There were scenes that nearly broke my heart, and there were episodes that made <br />me laugh out loud. As the publishers might have described Kate Atkinson on the cover of the book: <br />a writer at the height of her powers. I loved this book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15076731263582756085" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Mary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15849662-mary"><img alt="Mary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1409142849p2/15849662.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1265516935?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary" href="/user/show/15849662-mary">Mary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1265516935" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1591404025746784356">I hated myself for not loving this book. I was stunned by Life after Life. I waited anxiously for this book. And while I did love many parts, I didn't like others. Atkinson is a wonderful writer, her characters are all interesting and unique. Her stories are quite compelling, disturbing, historically perfect. I ate up everything, except for the actual war stories. While Teddy was flying, piloting, I found myself skipping over those parts. They held no interest for me, and I was mad at myself but</span>
  <span id="freeText1591404025746784356" style="display:none">I hated myself for not loving this book. I was stunned by Life after Life. I waited anxiously for this book. And while I did love many parts, I didn't like others. Atkinson is a wonderful writer, her characters are all interesting and unique. Her stories are quite compelling, disturbing, historically perfect. I ate up everything, except for the actual war stories. While Teddy was flying, piloting, I found myself skipping over those parts. They held no interest for me, and I was mad at myself but I couldn't read them. I felt like I was watching an old black and white war movie. Which I suppose, is actually a compliment because that's kind of what it was. <br /><br />Every single character was wonderful in their own way. I loved Teddy. I cheered for him, I cried for him. While I was a bit disappointed in spots, there were others that were miraculous, that made my heart soar. The ending gave me goosebumps.<br /><br />I wish I could give this 4 stars, I know I am in the minority here, but much of the reading was almost a chore. I'm glad I finished it, and I definitely would recommend it. I just didn't love it.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1529785167" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1529785167" />
    <a title="Barbara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2172096-barbara"><img alt="Barbara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598060221p2/2172096.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1529785167?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara" href="/user/show/2172096-barbara">Barbara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1529785167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7563425645756143956"><br/><b> 3.5 stars </b> <br/><br/>This second book in the 'Todd Family' saga concentates on Teddy Todd. It works well as a standalone. <br/><br/>*****<br/><br/>"A God in Ruins" revolves around Teddy Todd, who - in a nutshell - grew up in the English countryside, was the apple of his mother's eye, lost his loving father, was close to his sister Ursula, became a bomber pilot in WWII, married his childhood sweetheart, worked as a journalist, had a horrid narcissistic daughter, helped raise his grandchildren, got old, and died. <br/><br/>The book rep</span>
  <span id="freeText7563425645756143956" style="display:none"><br /><b> 3.5 stars </b> <br /><br />This second book in the 'Todd Family' saga concentates on Teddy Todd. It works well as a standalone. <br /><br />*****<br /><br />"A God in Ruins" revolves around Teddy Todd, who - in a nutshell - grew up in the English countryside, was the apple of his mother's eye, lost his loving father, was close to his sister Ursula, became a bomber pilot in WWII, married his childhood sweetheart, worked as a journalist, had a horrid narcissistic daughter, helped raise his grandchildren, got old, and died. <br /><br />The book repeatedly pings back and forth in time, covering incidents from Teddy's childhood to his very old age. Overall, we see Teddy as a happy child, growing up and going to college, traveling in Europe, joining the RAF, dropping bombs on Germany. becoming a POW, having a relatively good marriage, dealing with a difficult daughter, encouraging a seemingly inept grandson, taking a nostalgic trip with his grown granddaughter, moving to assisted living, dying in a nursing home.....and lots of things in between.<br /><br />The book doesn't lend itself to review as a story with a linear plot so I'll just highlight aspects that stood out for me.<br /><br />Some of the most vivid scenes in the book describe Teddy and his bomber team (navigator, radio operator, bomb aimer, gunners, etc.) on their forays into enemy territory. The relatively slow, lumbering bombers took off from an English airfield, flew in the dark - often for many hours - and were easy targets for both groundfire and enemy fighter planes. Moreover, to Teddy's chagrin (as he found out after the war) the early bombers were often wildly inaccurate, blitzing civilians rather than the intended industrial targets. The author's deft writing brings Teddy's RAF companions to life, and I liked and sympathized with them as they fought and often died. <br /><br />In the course of the story Teddy has a philosophical discussion with his sister about the morality of bombing Germany to smithereens. Teddy seems to feel no guilt about this, apparently believing that all's fair in war and you do what you have to do. For me, this seems like an understandable attitude but other readers may feel differently. <br /><br />After the war,Teddy marries his fiancé Nancy and they have a daughter named Viola. Little Viola is a sweet child, very attached to her mom. Sadly, Nancy dies young and Viola blames Teddy, is overwhelmed with grief, and - perhaps because of this (but who knows) - evolves into a selfish, self-absorbed woman, oblivious to the needs of others. Viola is almost a caricature of a 'flower child', taking up a hippie lifestyle, living on communes, becoming a vegetarian, and having one boyfriend (or husband) after another. Viola eventually has a son she calls 'Sunny' and a daughter she calls 'Moon', being too carefree (or lazy) to choose conventional names. Moon, who renames herself 'Bertie', is an insightful child that has Viola's number from the get-go.<br /><br />In any case, Viola soon abandons her children, leaving them in Teddy's care. In one of the more disturbing sections of the book Viola sends 7-year-old Sunny to live with his bipolar father's family. The poor little tyke's 'grandmama' belongs to the neglectful school of child-rearing. She provides no love or nurturing, very little food, and criticizes Sunny (who she calls Philip) constantly. Grandmama is also determined to send little Sunny to boarding school so he can grow up and continue the family name. Sunny is miserable but gets his own back a bit when - prevented from reaching the bathroom in time by Grandmama - he takes a dump on the living room carpet (ha ha ha). <br /><br />It's also clear that Viola has little love or time for her father Teddy who - especially when he gets on in years - she regards as a burden and annoyance. Mostly Viola seems to covet Teddy's more valuable possessions.<br /><br />Viola eventually becomes a successful novelist and makes some half-hearted attempts to reconnect with her children, but it's too little too late. Bertie's attitude toward her mom is especially knowing and sardonic and Sunny takes off for foreign shores.<br /><br />Teddy himself comes across as a handsome, affable, intelligent man who tries to live a good life and be a good person. As a youth, however, Teddy has a rather flexible moral code. Even though he's engaged to his sweetheart Nancy during WWII, Teddy has no hesitation about romance and sex with other women, the apparent explanation being that pilots had a very short life expectancy. Still, after the war Teddy is a loyal husband and loving grandpa who's largely responsible for the happiness and success his grandchildren eventually achieve.<br /><br />In lighter parts of the story, Teddy - as a child - is the model for his Aunt Izzy's series of children's books about 'Augustus', a hilariously naughty little boy. The tales of Augustus sprinkled through the book are fun and entertaining. Teddy also has several dogs as the story unfolds, and one sweet pooch serves as the mascot for his bomber team. I got a kick out of these endearing pets.<br /> <br />I expected to really like this book, which has garnered high praise from critics and readers. And I do think the book is well-written with characters that are vivid and believable. Still, I didn't enjoy the book as much as I'd hoped. It felt too long and slow-moving and I found myself wishing it was finished.<br /><br />Still, I recommend the book to fans of literary fiction.<br /><br />You can follow my reviews at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot.com/">https://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot....</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="7563425645756143956" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1227189793" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1227189793" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1826682-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252295186p2/1826682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1227189793?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/1826682-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=art">art</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=funny">funny</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=parenting">parenting</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=buddhism">buddhism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=historical-novel">historical-novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1227189793" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13292066719635812378">Atkinson returns to her theme of “What If” in this companion volume to <i>Life After Life</i> in which we met Ursula Todd and many possible iterations of her life. Ursula’s brother Teddy and his family take center stage in this novel and the generation gap is caught beautifully as Atkinson articulates the view from Ted and that of his only daughter, Viola. Viola’s son and daughter, Sunny and Bertie, exhibit an equal distance from understanding their own mother’s life choices and personality. <br/><br/>Kate Atkin</span>
  <span id="freeText13292066719635812378" style="display:none">Atkinson returns to her theme of “What If” in this companion volume to <i>Life After Life</i> in which we met Ursula Todd and many possible iterations of her life. Ursula’s brother Teddy and his family take center stage in this novel and the generation gap is caught beautifully as Atkinson articulates the view from Ted and that of his only daughter, Viola. Viola’s son and daughter, Sunny and Bertie, exhibit an equal distance from understanding their own mother’s life choices and personality. <br /><br />Kate Atkinson adds an <i>Author’s Note</i> at the end of this story which tells us much of what she was communicating in this novel. “It is about fiction,” she tells us. “We must imagine what we cannot know.” Atkinson chose to look at the Second World War, and makes her central character an RAF airman. Teddy, we wonder at the end, does he even exist? That’s the thing about fiction. All that attention, care, and love lavished, and in the end, they are only characters on paper. But Atkinson caught enough truth in her writing that we know these characters, Ted, Viola, Sunny, are real enough within each of us. <br /><br />What I like best about Atkinson’s work is her sense of humor about the tragedies of human life. She is a wonderful storyteller on the order of a Bruegel painting: large canvas, detailed figures not all doing the good and great thing. She gives us history, and we see the now, but we also get potential for the future. She does not leave us feeling that the thoughtful or erudite must be gloomy or humorless. With this two-book fiction, she gives us enough distance to see possible outcomes of decisions made now, and we always have the sense that we can change the outcomes if we don’t like where we’re headed. Her characters are complex enough to exhibit the petty and the grand. Once again, we always have a sense of possibility: character is malleable! We can change.<br /><br />Atkinson tells us that this novel is “about fiction…and the Fall of Man from grace.” She indicates many references in the novel to Utopia, the Garden, the Way and then the falling, rising pattern of the characters, birds, planes correspond to what she is trying to convey. War is a fall from grace. And no matter how we frame the argument, innocents become victims. Atkinson reiterates that she is not a polemicist so wants her characters instead to express doubt and insecurity about the notion of the efficacy of war.<br /><br />The thing about Atkinson’s fiction is that it is capacious enough to include the traditions of the past with the irresistible freshness and piquancy and social criticism of now. She does a marvelous job of telling a rip-snorting story at the same time she is urging caution. She was always a wonderful writer of fiction. She is in the process of becoming a great one. <br /><br />A word about the audio production of this title: Hachette Audio did a brilliant job of producing this audio, read with terrific understanding by Alex Jennings. It is highly recommended. However, if one is familiar with Kate Atkinson's work, one will note that Atkinson has a tendency to move easily and quickly forward and back in time and between outlooks of various characters. One shift that was particularly difficult for me to catch in the audio was the story of Augustus. At the end, when I realized the importance of Augustus, I had to admit I didn't understand where he fit in. One glance at the paper copy set things straight for me since Augustus' story is set in different type in paper.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13292066719635812378" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1375685573" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1375685573" />
    <a title="Ellie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2908629-ellie"><img alt="Ellie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399799985p2/2908629.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1375685573?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellie" href="/user/show/2908629-ellie">Ellie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=2015indchallenge">2015indchallenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2908629-ellie?shelf=family">family</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1375685573" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1981057134690640219"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10015.Kate_Atkinson' title='Kate Atkinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Kate Atkinson</a> is one of my favorite authors. I love her Jackson Brody detective series. I loved the quirky <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life' title='Life After Life by Kate Atkinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Life After Life</a> in which the heroine, Ursula, keeps dying and coming back to life. Not being much of a reader of war stories (with exceptions such as the brilliant <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/133518.The_Things_They_Carried' title='The Things They Carried by Tim O'Brien' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Things They Carried</a>) I was apprehensive about <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3722183.A_God_in_Ruins' title='A God in Ruins by Kate Atkinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>A God in Ruins</a>, the story of Ursula's beloved brother Teddy, described as a companion novel to Life After Life. The book tells the story of Teddy's experience as a fighter pilot d</span>
  <span id="freeText1981057134690640219" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10015.Kate_Atkinson" title="Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Kate Atkinson</a> is one of my favorite authors. I love her Jackson Brody detective series. I loved the quirky <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life" title="Life After Life by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Life After Life</a> in which the heroine, Ursula, keeps dying and coming back to life. Not being much of a reader of war stories (with exceptions such as the brilliant <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/133518.The_Things_They_Carried" title="The Things They Carried by Tim O'Brien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Things They Carried</a>) I was apprehensive about <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3722183.A_God_in_Ruins" title="A God in Ruins by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">A God in Ruins</a>, the story of Ursula's beloved brother Teddy, described as a companion novel to Life After Life. The book tells the story of Teddy's experience as a fighter pilot during World War II. <br /><br />As my five star rating probably shows, my worry was groundless. I adored <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3722183.A_God_in_Ruins" title="A God in Ruins by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">A God in Ruins</a>, which I read somewhat breathlessly in two sittings (I was worried that I wouldn't finish this 553 page novel in the week's time I had from the library. Clearly, I worry too much.) There's a lot about Teddy's life before and after the war, a lot of the workings of family and relationships, maybe more than there is about the war. But the war and Teddy's role in it are vividly described, and I found those pages both gripping and often harrowing.<br /><br />Atkinson clearly admires and supports the young men (boys, really) who fought the war but she doesn't ignore the bombing of civilians, a deliberate campaign that may or may not have actually helped the Allies win the war. However, she never questions the heroism of the soldiers, here represented by the RAF. I felt especially close to the story as my father was a gunner in the American air force. Reading Teddy's story, I understood why my father talked about his war experiences sparingly, focusing on his time between flights.<br /><br />I rarely feel as involved in the lives of a book's characters, not since I was a child have I felt as passionately about fictional people as I did with this book. I felt angry on Teddy's behalf at his daughter Viola's constant rejection of him. This is no spoiler; as with so much of this book, we find out much about Teddy and Viola's relationship at the outset. Many critical events are revealed early in the story, long before they actually occur in the timeline of the story. <br /><br />The book is written in sections that jump around chronologically so that a chapter set in 1947 may follow or be followed by events taking place in 1993, 2011, or 1925. This reduces some, but not all, plot surprises.<br /><br />The end of the novel is a serious shock and probably will make-or-break readers' ultimate response to the novel. I was shocked and a little angry but it did intrigue me.<br /><br />Ultimately, I loved this novel and its characters (even the awful Viola, Teddy's daughter). I highly recommend this book to anyone who appreciates excellent writing and passionate story-telling (it's also amazingly well researched). It made me think about second World War more deeply and its cost (and all the other wars we fight) to everyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1981057134690640219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1311469697" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1311469697" />
    <a title="switterbug (Betsey)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2843912-switterbug-betsey"><img alt="switterbug (Betsey)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374315754p2/2843912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1311469697?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="switterbug (Betsey)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="switterbug (Betsey)" href="/user/show/2843912-switterbug-betsey">switterbug (Betsey)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2843912-switterbug-betsey?shelf=favorite-books-i-have-reviewed">favorite-books-i-have-reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1311469697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3747557164837312798">“The dead were legion, and remembrance was a kind of duty…not always related to love.”<br/><br/>This is Atkinson’s companion piece (her words) to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life' title='Life After Life by Kate Atkinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Life After Life</a>, the story of the privileged Todd family in Fox Corner, in England, with WW II as the highlight of events. A GOD IN RUINS could be read without having read the previous novel, but it has so much more meaning if you read them both. This is a tour de force, and after having closed the last page in tears, and meditated on this astounding book, perha</span>
  <span id="freeText3747557164837312798" style="display:none">“The dead were legion, and remembrance was a kind of duty…not always related to love.”<br /><br />This is Atkinson’s companion piece (her words) to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life" title="Life After Life by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Life After Life</a>, the story of the privileged Todd family in Fox Corner, in England, with WW II as the highlight of events. A GOD IN RUINS could be read without having read the previous novel, but it has so much more meaning if you read them both. This is a tour de force, and after having closed the last page in tears, and meditated on this astounding book, perhaps I am ready to attempt some sort of review, or at least some reflections. However, I feel so inadequate to convey the brilliance of this luminous novel. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life" title="Life After Life by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Life After Life</a> focused on the upbringing of the five Todd children, especially Ursula (the third), who, via Atkinson’s effective device, died and was reborn (as Ursula again) many times. These numerous iterations of Ursula didn’t come across as contrived, as it didn’t depend on the device itself to execute the theme and heft of the story. Rather, Ursula’s many lives deepened her character, and also elucidated how civilization views, revises, and categorizes history, and how the construct of memories are as impermanent as a day. <br /><br />It is the ephemeral nature of life, and our construct of memories in making the chronicle of past events permanent, that are explored so well in both these novels. There are many themes that abound in the Todd family struggles—love, family, authenticity, loss, renewal, and perception, among others. But, what struck me most was the outcome of a battered era, when war is over--missed connections, misunderstandings, and isolation. How to live a future when you are carrying a burden of a damaged past? <br /><br />Atkinson pulls off an epic tale of history, seen through the eyes of one family, with compelling genre-bending talent. This book focuses on Ursula’s brother, Teddy, an RAF bomber during WW II--the squadron leader--fierce, courageous, heroic---who saw war in all its savagery, as he bombed whole cities and watched them burn from an aerial distance. There were times when the raw beauty of the flames down below were poetic—what a paradox—how killing the innocent had a feral beauty to it. <br /><br />“The coloured lights were joined by the bright quick flashes of the high explosives…and everywhere there was the enchanting twinkling of white lights as thousands upon thousands of incendiaries rained down on the city.” (Hamburg burning)<br /><br />Both Teddy and Ursula understood the complex dualities of war--enough to understand that, in order to stay alive, and fulfill your duty as a military servant, there can be no equivocating in the field--or in the air. Only later, when it is history, can the lens shift to judgment. Judging in hindsight wreaks the havoc of guilt and the righteousness of war. Teddy, postwar, was a diminished self--quiet, passive, accepting, but full of kindness and generosity. Unfortunately, he couldn't convey his feelings with any passion, and the war figure of Teddy was subsumed and mostly forgotten. Moreover, Teddy's love for his wife and childhood sweetheart, Nancy, lacked passion. But he was devoted to her, with a robust dependability.<br /><br />The metaphor of war--its brutal, tyrannizing power--can also be assigned to the seismic misunderstanding in families--in this case, Teddy's daughter, Viola, who was in a solitary battle with her father. Once her mother died, she felt abandoned and betrayed, and, although she herself abandoned her children, (several times) for her selfish quests, she wouldn't forgive her father for being the parent who stayed alive to raise her.<br /><br />Atkinson’s postmodern-ish narrative is told through the lens of time periods and generations regularly juxtaposed, the structure of which would fall apart in the hands of a lesser author. She splices time into slivers, really, yet seamlessly segues and alternates time so that when one passage jumps a generation back and forth, it gives weight not just to what is happening now, but what happened before, and what will occur in the future. The author allows us to see particular scenes from multi-perspectives—not just from different characters, but sometimes one character remembering multiple times, at a later or earlier occasion. Sublime.<br /><br />Atkinson also winks at us with references to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life" title="Life After Life by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Life After Life</a>. Certain events that happened offstage in LAL are illuminated here, and she executes the inverse flawlessly, also; she periodically pulls us offstage in her new book with nods to major events from LAL. The past and present are wrapped around each other, days of future passed. Time is not a banner laid out linearly, but rather a gossamer and vertical mass of bridged and dissonant memories.<br /><br />Teddy lives to be almost a century old (as we learn early on), and throughout the novel, the narrative see-saws from Teddy the child, in the mid 1920s, to Teddy the Bomber pilot during the war, later as a husband and father, then a loving grandfather, and then as a dying old man. Even though we know the outline of the future—Atkinson purposely telegraphs events—I was emotionally blitzed by her phenomenal storytelling, as she fills in the spaces of scenarios and allows us to see deeper into her characters.<br /><br />This will undoubtedly go down as one of my top 25 novels of a lifetime. I don't think that Kate Atkinson is finished with the Todd family yet. There's Bertie (Roberta) and Sunny, Teddy's beloved grandchildren, and then there are the lives of those that were only casually referenced. The author may choose to place them on center stage. I can't imagine Teddy as an offhand character; however, the author is a wizard at pulling her characters into the light, or dissolving them into the darkness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3747557164837312798" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
