<div id="review_1340174169" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1340174169" />
    <a title="PattyMacDotComma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1128612-pattymacdotcomma"><img alt="PattyMacDotComma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577835191p2/1128612.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1340174169?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="PattyMacDotComma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="PattyMacDotComma" href="/user/show/1128612-pattymacdotcomma">PattyMacDotComma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=aa-ce">aa-ce</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=award-win-listed">award-win-listed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=aa">aa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1340174169" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17877773833551659402"><b>“As you got older and time went on, you realized that the distinction between truth and fiction didn’t really matter because eventually everything disappeared into the soupy, amnesiac mess of history. Personal or political, it made no difference.”</b><br/><br/><b>4★</b><br/>What if? Almost exactly a year ago, I enjoyed Ursula’s repeating “What if?” story in Atkinson’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life' title='Life After Life by Kate Atkinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Life After Life</a>, (which I reviewed here <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/847303805'>https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> and I was looking forward to this about brother Teddy. But I was more than</span>
  <span id="freeText17877773833551659402" style="display:none"><b>“As you got older and time went on, you realized that the distinction between truth and fiction didn’t really matter because eventually everything disappeared into the soupy, amnesiac mess of history. Personal or political, it made no difference.”</b><br /><br /><b>4★</b><br />What if? Almost exactly a year ago, I enjoyed Ursula’s repeating “What if?” story in Atkinson’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life" title="Life After Life by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Life After Life</a>, (which I reviewed here <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/847303805">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> and I was looking forward to this about brother Teddy. But I was more than halfway through before I really got interested in Teddy and his family. What was the truth? Which character do I believe? <br /><br />Of course, overshadowing everything is why do we not learn from the horrors of previous wars? <br /><br />I trusted Atkinson’s eccentric storytelling, so I kept reading. She manages family dynamics so well, and I enjoy her off-beat humour that pops up unexpectedly.<br /><br /><b>“Sylvie removed her fur and put on her light evening duster for which only hard-working silkworms had been sacrificed.”<br />. . . <br />“When he came back outside, into the harsh daylight, the dog sniffed him suspiciously for signs of infidelity.<br />. . . <br /><i>‘Surprise?’</i> she said when she eventually tracked him down. <i>‘It is,’</i> he said. They had hugged warily, as if one of them might have a knife.<br />. . . <br />“She had sent an email to her agent and asked her to tell people that she was having an operation (she was, she was having her mind removed) and to make her apologies all round.”</b><br /><br />Viewpoints change. We hear from Teddy’s parents, Teddy, Nancy, Violet and others. Teddy grew up loving nature and picked flowers for his adoring, glamorous mother (Sylvie, of the fur and silk).<br /><br />The British were hammered in both World Wars, and Teddy’s childhood was coloured by it. His father, Hugh, who <b>“retired to his growlery again with a small glass of whisky and the stub of a half-smoked cigar”</b>, once told a neighbour that<br /><br /><b>“Sylvie had no intention of discussing the war. It had been a rip in the fabric of their lives and she had sewn it up neatly. ‘Oh, that’s a very good way of putting it, Hugh,’ Mrs Shawcross – Roberta – said. ‘But, you know, unless you can do very good invisible stitching there’ll always be a scar, won’t there?’ He regretted introducing the needlework metaphors.”</b><br /><br />I enjoyed the gradual peeling back of the layers of each character’s understanding of each other. I had decided too quickly what each person was like or was capable of. I still don’t like her, but I understand why Teddy’s and Nancy’s only child Viola appears to be a distasteful spoiled brat who rebels when she grows up and moves to a commune with a disreputable character and has two kids: Sun and Moon. <br /><br /><b>“He loved Viola as only a parent can love a child, but it was hard work.”</b><br /><br />And I understand Teddy, who faced war so young and did his best to deal with it. But Nancy, at home, didn’t appreciate the price he paid.<br /><br /><b>“His aircraft had been coned and shot down by flak on the dreadful raid to Nuremberg. He hadn’t known it at the time but it was the worst night of the war for Bomber Command – ninety-six aircraft lost, five hundred and forty-five men killed, more than in the whole Battle of Britain. But by the time he made it home this was all old, cold news, Nuremberg all but forgotten. <i>‘You were very brave,’</i> Nancy said, with the same encouraging indifference – to Teddy’s ears anyway – that she might have afforded him if he had done well in a maths test.</b><br /><br />And I admire Atkinson, who can write a companion piece to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life" title="Life After Life by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Life After Life</a> which offers us the WHAT IFS? that make us think. I hope I’m not doing her a disservice by quoting from the end of the book, just to remind us, lest we forget:<br /><br /><b>“Fifty-five thousand, five hundred and seventy-three dead from Bomber Command. Seven million German dead, including the five hundred thousand killed by the Allied bombing campaign. The sixty million dead overall of the Second World War, including eleven million murdered in the Holocaust. The sixteen million of the First World War, over four million in Vietnam, forty million to the Mongol conquests, three and a half million to the Hundred Years War, the fall of Rome took seven million, the Napoleonic Wars took four million, twenty million to the Taiping Rebellion. And so on and so on and so on, all the way back to the Garden when Cain killed Abel.”</b><br /><br />The only reason it isn't 5 stars from me is because it took me so long to get it, if I can put it that way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17877773833551659402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1304218368" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8675092570451319023">Kate Atkinson kills me again. I'd write a review if I wasn't so bitter & broken-hearted that I'm not still immersed in Teddy's world. <br/><br/>(But review-ish: this is just barely not quite the soul-crusher that <i>Life After Life</i> was just because Viola exists in the book & she's so utterly awful & self-absorbed & goddammit just totally awful that every time she came up on the page I got yoinked right out of the story with extreme annoyance. And I liked reading about Bertie from everyone else's perspective,</span>
  <span id="freeText8675092570451319023" style="display:none">Kate Atkinson kills me again. I'd write a review if I wasn't so bitter &amp; broken-hearted that I'm not still immersed in Teddy's world. <br /><br />(But review-ish: this is just barely not quite the soul-crusher that <i>Life After Life</i> was just because Viola exists in the book &amp; she's so utterly awful &amp; self-absorbed &amp; goddammit just totally awful that every time she came up on the page I got yoinked right out of the story with extreme annoyance. And I liked reading about Bertie from everyone else's perspective, but I could have done without her chapter - yes, Bertie, I didn't like that guy you dated either &amp; I really didn't want to read about him or the seminar you were at. But except for Viola, this is book is deadly. I could seriously almost cry that I have nothing more in front of me to read about Teddy &amp; WWII. Atkinson can write WWII like no one else. Brilliant).</span>

<div id="review_1234702702" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1234702702" />
    <a title="Connie G" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g"><img alt="Connie G" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307502493p2/5364077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1234702702?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Connie G" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Connie G" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g">Connie G</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=england">england</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=world-war-ii">world-war-ii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=aviation">aviation</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1234702702" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13734409273692525437"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3722183.A_God_in_Ruins' title='A God in Ruins by Kate Atkinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>A God in Ruins</a> is a companion novel to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life' title='Life After Life by Kate Atkinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Life After Life</a>, the story of Ursula Todd who expired and was reborn multiple times during the first half of the 20th Century. Her brother Teddy was a RAF pilot during World War II who was shot down and missing in action. In <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3722183.A_God_in_Ruins' title='A God in Ruins by Kate Atkinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>A God in Ruins</a> we learn that he had been in a German POW camp for two years before returning to England.<br/><br/>The book is written with a constant shifting back and forth in time. The story starts slowly with incidents about Teddy's childhood </span>
  <span id="freeText13734409273692525437" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3722183.A_God_in_Ruins" title="A God in Ruins by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">A God in Ruins</a> is a companion novel to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15790842.Life_After_Life" title="Life After Life by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Life After Life</a>, the story of Ursula Todd who expired and was reborn multiple times during the first half of the 20th Century. Her brother Teddy was a RAF pilot during World War II who was shot down and missing in action. In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3722183.A_God_in_Ruins" title="A God in Ruins by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">A God in Ruins</a> we learn that he had been in a German POW camp for two years before returning to England.<br /><br />The book is written with a constant shifting back and forth in time. The story starts slowly with incidents about Teddy's childhood and old age. His wife, their daughter Viola, and his grandchildren are also important in this multi-generational saga. Viola is a selfish woman who neglects her children. She goes from an unorganized commune to other bad situations looking for fulfillment. She was a witness to an event that Teddy always kept secret, and it drove a wedge between them. Viola is almost overdrawn as a character, appearing totally unlikable.<br /><br />Teddy is a kind, stoic man who signs up for the RAF during World War II, finding his passion in flying bombers. The book picks up pace during the war years with very exciting, well-researched writing about the bombing runs. The story does not ignore the fact that many civilians were also killed as the bombs were dropped on their targets. Skill, a tight crew, and a lot of luck was involved in surviving as a RAF pilot. It's worth reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3722183.A_God_in_Ruins" title="A God in Ruins by Kate Atkinson" rel="nofollow noopener">A God in Ruins</a> just for the excellent chapters about Teddy's war years. The ending came with a twist that is a clever bit of storytelling, and fits in well with the spirit of the two books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13734409273692525437" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('OCl3dRzj8wkzX0zzCs1mA0cTMYIbxEVVW6z8cHaS0UaGZ1m06T1zcTcetCtiLF2TdyJ+RYuMK8nh4xKbx0FIcQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

His first order of business is to buy the Dutch House, a lavi<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText224196913998664044\" style=\"display:none\">At the end of the Second World War, Cyril Conroy combines luck and a single canny investment to begin an enormous real estate empire, propelling his family from poverty to enormous wealth. His first order of business is to buy the Dutch House, a lavish estate in the suburbs outside of Philadelphia. Meant as a surprise for his wife, the house sets in motion the undoing of everyone he loves.\n\nThe story is told by Cyril’s son Danny, as he and his older sister, the brilliantly acerbic and self-assured Maeve, are exiled from the house where they grew up by their stepmother. The two wealthy siblings are thrown back into the poverty their parents had escaped from and find that all they have to count on is one another. It is this unshakable bond between them that both saves their lives and thwarts their futures.\n\nSet over the course of five decades, The Dutch House is a dark fairy tale about two smart people who cannot overcome their past. Despite every outward sign of success, Danny and Maeve are only truly comfortable when they’re together. Throughout their lives, they return to the well-worn story of what they’ve lost with humor and rage. The blacksmith’s son from Putney emerges from the spring’s bloodbath to continue his climb to power and wealth, while his formidable master, Henry VIII, settles to short-lived happiness with his third queen before Jane dies giving birth to the male heir he most craves.\n\nCromwell is a man with only his wits to rely on; he has no great family to back him, no private army. Despite rebellion at home, traitors plotting abroad and the threat of invasion testing Henry’s regime to the breaking point, Cromwell’s robust imagination sees a new country in the mirror of the future. She traces the final years of Thomas Cromwell, the boy from nowhere who climbs to the heights of power, offering a defining portrait of predator and prey, of a ferocious contest between present and He tries to share his love and knowledge of things in nature with her, but she does not seem interested. Teddy is upset by the fact that Izzie claims to have eaten a skylark. Izzie asks him questions about his likes and dislikes. She later uses his answers to write a series of books loosely based on him even though the main character is nothing like Teddy. Teddy hates the books and the fact that they are dedicated to him.When World War II begins, Teddy signs up. This book takes a unique perspective on dramatic national events as well as ordinary life, as experienced by women down the centuries. From the saintly but severe medieval Queen Margaret to today’s first minister Nicola Sturgeon, it encompasses women from all stations of class and fame and notoriety, offering a tantalising view of what happened to them, and how they felt. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9177907019220087037">Kate Atkinson was born in York and now lives in Edinburgh. Her first novel, <i>Behind the Scenes at the Museum</i>, won the Whitbread Book of the Year Award and she has been a critically acclaimed international bestselling author ever since.<br/><br/>She is the author of a collection of short stories, <i>Not the End of the World</i>, and of the critically acclaimed novels <i>Human Croquet</i>, <i>Emotionally Weird</i>, <i>Case Histories</i>,</span>
  <span id="freeText9177907019220087037" style="display:none">Kate Atkinson was born in York and now lives in Edinburgh. Her first novel, <i>Behind the Scenes at the Museum</i>, won the Whitbread Book of the Year Award and she has been a critically acclaimed international bestselling author ever since.<br /><br />She is the author of a collection of short stories, <i>Not the End of the World</i>, and of the critically acclaimed novels <i>Human Croquet</i>, <i>Emotionally Weird</i>, <i>Case Histories</i>, and <i>One Good Turn</i>.<br /><br /><i>Case Histories</i> introduced her readers to Jackson Brodie, former police inspector turned private investigator, and won the Saltire Book of the Year Award and the Prix Westminster. <br /><br />When Will There Be Good News? was voted Richard &amp; Judy Book Best Read of the Year. After Case Histories and One Good Turn, it was her third novel to feature the former private detective Jackson Brodie, who makes a welcome return in Started Early, Took My Dog. </span>
